getText:java.lang.String:<?xml version="1.0" encoding="UTF-8"?>&n<Data xmlns="http://www.digitalmeasures.com/schema/data" dmd:date="2022-01-21" xmlns:dmd="http://www.digitalmeasures.com/schema/data-metadata">&n	<Record dmd:surveyId="11977669" userId="1466528" username="ezs921">&n		<dmd:IndexEntry entryKey="FT" indexKey="ADMIN_FTPT" text="FT"/>&n		<dmd:IndexEntry entryKey="Professor" indexKey="ADMIN_RANK" text="Professor"/>&n		<dmd:IndexEntry entryKey="Tenured" indexKey="ADMIN_TENURE" text="Tenured"/>&n		<dmd:IndexEntry entryKey="College for Health, Community and Policy" indexKey="COLLEGE" text="College for Health, Community and Policy"/>&n		<dmd:IndexEntry entryKey="Demography" indexKey="DEPARTMENT" text="Demography"/>&n		<PCI dmd:created="2013-07-29T02:00:22" dmd:lastModified="2019-11-04T06:56:43" dmd:lastModifiedSource="SYSTEM" dmd:originalSource="IMPORT" id="75861690368">&n			<BANNER_ID>@01441749</BANNER_ID>&n			<EMPID>6001041047</EMPID>&n			<PIDM>1360224</PIDM>&n			<PREFIX/>&n			<FNAME>Rene</FNAME>&n			<MNAME>M</MNAME>&n			<LNAME>Zenteno</LNAME>&n			<SUFFIX/>&n			<FULL_NAME/>&n			<EMAIL>RENE.ZENTENO@UTSA.EDU</EMAIL>&n			<BUILDING>MONTEREY BUILDING</BUILDING>&n			<ROOMNUM>4.450B</ROOMNUM>&n			<OPHONE1>210</OPHONE1>&n			<OPHONE2>458</OPHONE2>&n			<OPHONE3>3140</OPHONE3>&n			<DPHONE1>210</DPHONE1>&n			<DPHONE2>458</DPHONE2>&n			<DPHONE3>3163</DPHONE3>&n			<FAX1>210</FAX1>&n			<FAX2>458</FAX2>&n			<FAX3>3164</FAX3>&n			<WEBSITE>http://copp.utsa.edu/demography/</WEBSITE>&n			<DTM_DOB>November</DTM_DOB>&n			<DTD_DOB>27</DTD_DOB>&n			<DTY_DOB>1962</DTY_DOB>&n			<DOB_START>1962-11-27</DOB_START>&n			<DOB_END>1962-11-27</DOB_END>&n			<GENDER>Male</GENDER>&n			<ETHNICITY>Hispanic or Latino</ETHNICITY>&n			<RACE>White</RACE>&n			<CITIZEN>No</CITIZEN>&n			<BIO>René Zenteno is currently professor of Demography in the College of Public Policy at the University of Texas at San Antonio. &n&nHe was the first vice provost for international initiatives at UTSA. During this time he was also the founding executive director of ConTex, a joint initiative of the University of Texas System and Mexico’s National Council of Science and Technology (CONACYT). As vice provost, he led efforts to advance the global profile and reputation of UTSA by: more than doubled the number of students participating in educational abroad experiences; expanding UTSA research collaborations and exchange programs for faculty and students with top ranked universities in Latin America, particularly Mexico, Brazil and Chile, as well as China, Japan, India, France and Germany; leading the first accreditation of UTSA’s Intensive English Program; and adopting streamlined procedures for travel to restricted regions. As executive director of ConTex, Zenteno spearheaded the launch of a $16 million five-year investment committed to fostering collaboration between the United States and Mexico through graduate training, research and discovery.&n&nZenteno served as the Under Secretary in the Ministry of the Interior in Mexico from 2010-2012. As Under Secretary, he oversaw all policies, programs and activities related to population, migration, and religious affairs. He was instrumental in writing, negotiating and enacting the 2011 Mexican Immigration Law. He also helped pass the Law on Refugees and Complementary Protection. These legal instruments provide basic rights to migrants and refugees in Mexico regardless of immigration status.&n&nPrior to joining the UTSA faculty, Zenteno served as provost and professor at El Colegio de la Frontera Norte and as executive director of the Center for U.S.-Mexican Studies at the University of California, San Diego. He also spent eight years on the faculty of the Monterrey Institute of Technology and Higher Education. &n&nOver the span of his academic career, Zenteno has procured 6.5 million dollars in grants, and has authored more than 50 publications. Zenteno has received several professional honors, including President of the Mexican Society of Demography (SOMEDE) and membership in the National Academy of Science of Mexico. He is a recipient of the Alfonso Reyes chair of the Institut des Hautes Etudes de l'Amérique latine at Université de la Sorbonne Nouvelle Paris III.&n&nZenteno has published widely in the areas of social and demographic change, international migration, and social inequality, with a focus on Mexico, U.S.-Mexican migration, and Mexican immigrant incorporation. Zenteno’s articles have been published in the Proceedings of the National Academic of Sciences, Social Problems, Journal of Development Economics, Latin American Research Review, International Migration Review, The Annals of the American Academy of Political and Social Science, Journal of Marriage and the Family, American Economic Journal: Applied Economics, Estudios Demográficos y Urbanos, Frontera Norte, and other academic journals.&n&nZenteno earned his bachelor’s degree in communications from Monterrey Institute of Technology and Higher Education, and his master’s degree in demography from El Colegio de Mexico. His doctorate is from the University of Texas at Austin in sociology and demography. He completed a two-year postdoctoral fellowship at the University of Pennsylvania. &n</BIO>&n			<TEACH_UNDERGRAD>Global Commerce &amp;amp; Human Mobility&nMarketing Research II&nSocio-cultural Values in Mexico and Latin America</TEACH_UNDERGRAD>&n			<TEACH_GRAD>International Migration&nPolicy Development&nGeneral Research Methods&nMexico: Development, Institutions and Policies&nEconomic Policy in Latin America&nQuantitative Methods&nDevelopment and Social Policy&nContemporary Sociology&nEpistemology&nInternational Migration&nResearch Methodology&nField Research&nStatistical Analysis of Survey Data&nMathematics and Research Methodology&nStatistics for Social Sciences&nSeminar on Social Methodology II&nPopulation and Society in Contemporary Mexico&nStatistics I&nApplied Statistics in Population Studies&nDemographic Analysis&nThesis Seminar&nSeminar on Migration I and II&nSeminar on Labor Force I and II&nSeminar on Population Change in Mexico&nDemographic Analysis&n&n&n&n&n</TEACH_GRAD>&n			<TEACHING_INTERESTS/>&n			<RESEARCH_INTERESTS>Migration is a major demographic force shaping the social, economic, political and cultural life of the United States and Mexico. Most of my research falls into three categories: 1) U.S.-Mexican migration, with special emphasis on its determinants and consequences for sending and receiving regions and immigrant adaptation, 2) immigration policy, undocumented migration and human rights and 3) social and demographic change in Mexico, including social mobility and family behavior. Underlying these foci is a common interest in issues of inequality, development, and stratification. A key tenet of my research is that social disparities in Mexico and the U.S. can be better understood by establishing comparisons between the two countries. This approach is relevant because millions of Mexicans today are shaping their life opportunities and those of their descendants by moving between two societies with clear divisions based on class and ethnicity. In order to understand the social inequality faced by Mexicans in both countries, I argue that we need to produce more systematic and comparable data and research on how characteristics of the family of origin are associated with educational, health and labor market outcomes on both sides of the border.</RESEARCH_INTERESTS>&n			<REFERENCES/>&n			<UPLOAD_PHOTO/>&n		</PCI>&n		<PASTHIST Scholarship</CLASSIFICATION>&n					<STATUS>Published</STATUS>&n					<TITLE>Mexican Adolescent Migration to the U.S. and Transitions to Adulthood</TITLE>&n					<TITLE_SECONDARY/>&n					<INTELLCONT_AUTH id="85482569729">&n						<FACULTY_NAME>1466528</FACULTY_NAME>&n						<FNAME>Rene</FNAME>&n						<MNAME>M</MNAME>&n						<LNAME>Zenteno</LNAME>&n						<STUDENT_LEVEL/>&n					</INTELLCONT_AUTH>&n					<INTELLCONT_AUTH id="85482569731">&n						<FACULTY_NAME/>&n						<FNAME>Edith</FNAME>&n						<MNAME/>&n						<LNAME>Guitérrez</LNAME>&n						<STUDENT_LEVEL/>&n					</INTELLCONT_AUTH>&n					<PUBLISHER>The Annals of the Science</PUBLISHER>&n					<PUBCTYST/>&n					<PUBCNTRY/>&n					<VOLUME>648</VOLUME>&n					<ISSUE>1</ISSUE>&n					<PAGENUM>18-37</PAGENUM>&n					<WEB_ADDRESS/>&n					<EDITORS/>&n					<ISBNISSN/>&n					<DOI/>&n					<AUDIENCE/>&n					<REFEREED>Yes</REFEREED>&n					<EDITORIAL_REVIEWED/>&n					<PUBLICAVAIL>Yes</PUBLICAVAIL>&n					<ACTIVITY_RANK/>&n					<ACTIVITY_RANK_OTHER/>&n					<AREA>Human and Social Development</AREA>&n					<ABSTRACT>Traditional theories used to analyze international migration fail to distinguish age-related variations on their key propositions. This paper contributes to our understanding of how the motivation for migrating varies depending on the stage in the life course, particularly during the youth-to-adult transition. Using data from the Mexican Migration Project, we estimate discrete-time-hazard models of the probabilities of a first migration using individual, household, community and macroeconomic variables during and after adolescence, and for both men and women. Results indicate that the determinants of migration operate differently during adolescence than later in the life course. While migration-related social capital has proved to be important factor in increasing and perpetuating migration, we found that its effect is even stronger for teenagers. We also shed light on how adolescent migration is influenced by other major markers of the transition to adulthood such as education, labor force experience and family formation.</ABSTRACT>&n					<FULL_TEXT/>&n					<DTM_START/>&n					<DTD_START/>&n					<DTY_START/>&n					<START_START/>&n					<START_END/>&n					<DTM_SUB/>&n					<DTD_SUB/>&n					<DTY_SUB/>&n					<SUB_START/>&n					<SUB_END/>&n					<DTM_EXPSUB/>&n					<DTD_EXPSUB/>&n					<DTY_EXPSUB/>&n					<EXPSUB_START/>&n					<EXPSUB_END/>&n					<DTM_ACC/>&n					<DTY_ACC/>&n					<ACC_START/>&n					<ACC_END/>&n					<DTM_PUB/>&n					<DTD_PUB/>&n					<DTY_PUB>2013</DTY_PUB>&n					<PUB_START>2013-01-01</PUB_START>&n					<PUB_END>2013-12-31</PUB_END>&n					<USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>Yes</USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>&n				</INTELLCONT>&n			</PUBS>&n			<PRESENT dmd:created="2021-07-23T17:38:40" dmd:endDate="2025-08-31" dmd:lastModified="2021-10-26T09:45:40" dmd:lastModifiedSource="SYSTEM" dmd:originalSource="IMPORT" dmd:primaryKey="10703" dmd:startDate="2020-09-01" id="222851072000">&n			<TYPE_CONGRANT>Proposal</TYPE_CONGRANT>&n			<PROPOSAL_ID>10703</PROPOSAL_ID>&n			<TYPE>Subaward</TYPE>&n			<TITLE>Individual, Household, and Contextual Determinants of Maternal Health among U.S. Latinas: A National Cross-sectional and Local Area Prospective Study PI's/Co-PI's;Sharedcredit: Zenteno, Rene PI - 100.00%</TITLE>&n			<SPONORG>University of Pennsylvania</SPONORG>&n			<CONGRANT_INVEST dmd:primaryKey="1466528|Rene|M|Zenteno" id="222851072001">&n				<FACULTY_NAME>1466528</FACULTY_NAME>&n				<FNAME>Rene</FNAME>&n				<MNAME>M</MNAME>&n				<LNAME>Zenteno</LNAME>&n			</CONGRANT_INVEST>&n			<ROLE_PI>Lead PD/PI</ROLE_PI>&n			<AMOUNT>176785.00</AMOUNT>&n			<ABSTRACT>Following a social determinants of health framework, the overall aim of our proposal is to systematically assess and compare risk factors for maternal mortality among Latina women in the U.S., paying specific attention to variation by nativity, country of origin, and geographic context. We take a mixed-method approach, combining national level secondary data analysis with a prospective community based participatory research study in Philadelphia, PA that will follow women during and after their pregnancy to collect high quality data on health risks and outcomes. This type of comprehensive account is necessary to identify sociocultural protective factors and areas of resilience that can inform public health interventions and grassroots organizations oriented towards mitigating negative pregnancy health outcomes among Latinas. Our specific aims are to: 1- Using birth certificate data, describe and model individual and household level differences in maternal risks and morbidities (i.e., gestational diabetes; hypertension eclampsia; transfusion) as well as the intervening mechanisms shaping maternal health (i.e., access and adequacy of prenatal care and health insurance coverage). We will compare results to NH White, Black, and Asian women, both U.S. and foreign-born. 2- Linking birth certificate data with county level information on immigration and socioeconomic characteristics obtained from the American Community Survey, to formulate multilevel models to assess how ethnic community characteristics (i.e. size, growth, and gender composition of the Latino community) and socioeconomic contexts affect maternal health. We will compare results to NH White, Black, and Asian women, both U.S. and foreign-born. 3- Linking birth certificate data with county level information on health care resources obtained primarily from the County Health Rankings ; Roadmap (CHR) and Small Area Health Insurance Estimates Projects (SAHIE), to formulate multilevel models to assess how health care context affects maternal health. We will compare results to NH White, Black, and Asian women, both U.S. and foreign-born. 4- Develop jointly with the Centre for the Culture, Art, Training, and Education (CCATE) a community-based participatory research approach to the identification of maternal health issues, data collection, information, and dissemination with the potential to serve newly emerging local communities receiving Latinas. 5- Apply a mixed method data collection approach that will prospectively collect a quantitative survey through respondent-driven sampling and qualitative in-depth interviews on health behaviors, health access, and maternal health among Latina women during and after pregnancy in Philadelphia, PA.</ABSTRACT>&n			<STATUS>Submitted</STATUS>&n			<DTM_SUB>May</DTM_SUB>&n			<DTD_SUB>26</DTD_SUB>&n			<DTY_SUB>2020</DTY_SUB>&n			<SUB_START>2020-05-26</SUB_START>&n			<SUB_END>2020-05-26</SUB_END>&n			<DTM_START>September</DTM_START>&n			<DTD_START>01</DTD_START>&n			<DTY_START>2020</DTY_START>&n			<START_START>2020-09-01</START_START>&n			<START_END>2020-09-01</START_END>&n			<DTM_END>August</DTM_END>&n			<DTD_END>31</DTD_END>&n			<DTY_END>2025</DTY_END>&n			<END_START>2025-08-31</END_START>&n			<END_END>2025-08-31</END_END>&n			<USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>Yes</USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>&n		</CONGRANT>&n		<CONGRANT dmd:created="2021-07-23T17:38:40" dmd:endDate="2019-05-31" dmd:lastModified="2021-10-26T09:45:40" dmd:lastModifiedSource="SYSTEM" dmd:originalSource="IMPORT" dmd:primaryKey="5996" dmd:startDate="2016-06-01" id="222851067904">&n			<TYPE_CONGRANT>Proposal</TYPE_CONGRANT>&n			<PROPOSAL_ID>5996</PROPOSAL_ID>&n			<TYPE>Grant</TYPE>&n			<TITLE>Strategies of Transnational Mexican Adolescents: Immigrant Agency, Social Networks, and Incorporation PI's/Co-PI's;Sharedcredit: Romo, Harriett PI - 100.00% Christiansen, Martha Sidury Co-PI - 0.00% Zenteno, Rene Co-PI - 0.00% Jones, Richard Co-PI - 0.00%</TITLE>&n			<SPONORG>Natl Science Fdn</SPONORG>&n			<CONGRANT_INVEST dmd:primaryKey="1466528|Rene|M|Zenteno" id="222851067905">&n				<FACULTY_NAME>1466528</FACULTY_NAME>&n				<FNAME>Rene</FNAME>&n				<MNAME>M</MNAME>&n				<LNAME>Zenteno</LNAME>&n			</CONGRANT_INVEST>&n			<ROLE_PI>Co-PD/PI</ROLE_PI>&n			<AMOUNT>1000000.00</AMOUNT>&n			<ABSTRACT>Overview We propose to identify and explain the experiences of transnational Mexican-origin adolescents (14-18 year-olds with at least one Mexican parent, who have migrated between Mexico and the US) as they attempt to adjust to institutions that inhibit their incorporation into the host society. We specifically focus on two target populationsâ€”Mexican undocumented adolescents in the United States, and US-born Mexican adolescents living in Mexico. The former often do not discover their legal status until they are 16-18 years old, when they â€œawaken to a nightmareâ€&amp;#157; (Gonzales and Chavez 2012) in which they are unable to get a driver;s license, work legally or enter a university. As a result their school performance, aspirations and economic incorporation suffer. In the case of US-born adolescents in Mexico, these youth have returned to Mexico voluntarily or involuntarily, usually with their parents, where they find that Mexican institutions are poorly equipped to accept them (ZÃºÃ±iga and Hamman 2009). Our research calls upon the theories of balance, social networks and incorporation to probe how adolescents cope with these obstacles within the context of discrimination, arbitrary application of federal policies, inadequate school and family resources, language proficiency and other constraints. Our fully integrated interdisciplinary team at the University of Texas at San Antonio, including co-PIs from Sociology, Geography, Education and Demography, has identified three urban areas in Texas (San Antonio, Austin, and Houston/Pasadena) and in Mexico (Toluca, Puebla, and Leon) that have high numbers of our two target populations. After analyzing key attributes from the American Community Survey and the Mexican Family Life Survey, we will locate schools and classes likely to have high concentrations of our target populations. We will survey students in their classes on basic demographic characteristics. We will conduct life history interviews with a subset of students, including questions on their social networks, attitudes and behaviors. The data will be coded and analyzed using both quantitative and narrative research methods.</ABSTRACT>&n			<STATUS>Submitted</STATUS>&n			<DTM_SUB>December</DTM_SUB>&n			<DTD_SUB>01</DTD_SUB>&n			<DTY_SUB>2015</DTY_SUB>&n			<SUB_START>2015-12-01</SUB_START>&n			<SUB_END>2015-12-01</SUB_END>&n			<DTM_START>June</DTM_START>&n			<DTD_START>01</DTD_START>&n			<DTY_START>2016</DTY_START>&n			<START_START>2016-06-01</START_START>&n			<START_END>2016-06-01</START_END>&n			<DTM_END>May</DTM_END>&n			<DTD_END>31</DTD_END>&n			<DTY_END>2019</DTY_END>&n			<END_START>2019-05-31</END_START>&n			<END_END>2019-05-31</END_END>&n			<USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>Yes</USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>&n		</CONGRANT>&n		<CONGRANT dmd:created="2021-07-23T17:38:40" dmd:endDate="2015-08-31" dmd:lastModified="2021-10-26T09:45:40" dmd:lastModifiedSource="SYSTEM" dmd:originalSource="IMPORT" dmd:primaryKey="4341" dmd:startDate="2014-09-01" id="222851069952">&n			<TYPE_CONGRANT>Award</TYPE_CONGRANT>&n			<PROPOSAL_ID>4341</PROPOSAL_ID>&n			<TYPE>Grant</TYPE>&n			<TITLE>The Impact of Migration and Immigration on Mexican and Mexican-American Adolescents: A Transnational Study PI's/Co-PI's;Sharedcredit: Romo, Harriett PI - 75.00% Zenteno, Rene Co-PI - 25.00%</TITLE>&n			<SPONORG>UTSA VPR Office</SPONORG>&n			<CONGRANT_INVEST dmd:primaryKey="1466528|Rene|M|Zenteno" id="222851069953">&n				<FACULTY_NAME>1466528</FACULTY_NAME>&n				<FNAME>Rene</FNAME>&n				<MNAME>M</MNAME>&n				<LNAME>Zenteno</LNAME>&n			</CONGRANT_INVEST>&n			<ROLE_PI>Co-PD/PI</ROLE_PI>&n			<AMOUNT>20000.00</AMOUNT>&n			<AWARDED>20000.00</AWARDED>&n			<FUNDING>5000.00</FUNDING>&n			<ABSTRACT>ABSTRACT. The proposed collaboration will develop research proposals that explore educational, health, identity, and behavioral implications for Mexican and Mexican-origin youth, both in Mexico and the United States, resulting from transnational relocations and separations under the new economic and political realities of Mexico-U.S. relations. Our study includes: 1) children (in Mexico) of deported Mexican parents, 2) children (in Mexico) of voluntary returnees, and 3) children who remain in the United States of Mexican parents. Many youth with Mexican immigrant parents have been born in the United States and hold dual nationality. We are interested in what happens to those U.S.-citizen children growing up in Mexico who may eventually return to the United States. While the focus is on the impact of migration, the study also includes a comparison group of Mexican American and Mexican-born immigrant children in San Antonio, Texas and their developmental trajectory in an Hispanic-majority U.S. city. The interdisciplinary project involves scholars from social work, sociology, geography, history, bicultural-bilingual studies, and demography to capture the complexity of migration experiences. Each of the team members has conducted empirical research related to Mexican migration and/or Mexican and Mexican American youth in the United States. The team will collect baseline data on return migration and location of these youth using existing studies and demographic/census reports in the United States and Mexico. Working with Mexican collaborators and drawing from the methodological expertise and research paradigms of the disciplines represented in the collaboration, the team will develop and field test survey and interview instruments focusing on the role of migration experiences in child and youth development, identity construction, citizenship, family relations, and peer socialization in distinct contexts (i.e., rural Mexico, a Mexican northern-border city, a Southern Mexico indigenous community, and San Antonio, Texas). Our goal is to develop several research proposals to be submitted to foundations and agencies funding research on youth and migration processes. Each of the Department Chairs represented by the collaborators has agreed to provide additional support for the collaboration, including research assistance, travel funds, and supplies. The UTSA Mexico Center will house the collaboration.</ABSTRACT>&n			<STATUS>Funded</STATUS>&n			<DTM_SUB>February</DTM_SUB>&n			<DTD_SUB>15</DTD_SUB>&n			<DTY_SUB>2014</DTY_SUB>&n			<SUB_START>2014-02-15</SUB_START>&n			<SUB_END>2014-02-15</SUB_END>&n			<DTM_START>September</DTM_START>&n			<DTD_START>01</DTD_START>&n			<DTY_START>2014</DTY_START>&n			<START_START>2014-09-01</START_START>&n			<START_END>2014-09-01</START_END>&n			<DTM_END>August</DTM_END>&n			<DTD_END>31</DTD_END>&n			<DTY_END>2015</DTY_END>&n			<END_START>2015-08-31</END_START>&n			<END_END>2015-08-31</END_END>&n			<USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>Yes</USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>&n		</CONGRANT>&n		<INTELLCONT Scholarship</CLASSIFICATION>&n			<PUBLICATION_LEVEL/>&n			<STATUS>Submitted</STATUS>&n			<TITLE>Evolving Trends of Latin American Immigration in the United States: Challenges for the Integration of Skilled Immigrants</TITLE>&n			<TITLE_SECONDARY>Economic Migration to the United States</TITLE_SECONDARY>&n			<INTELLCONT_AUTH id="225548115969">&n				<FACULTY_NAME>1466528</FACULTY_NAME>&n				<FNAME>Rene</FNAME>&n				<MNAME>M</MNAME>&n				<LNAME>Zenteno</LNAME>&n				<STUDENT_LEVEL/>&n				<ROLE/>&n			</INTELLCONT_AUTH>&n			<PUBLISHER>New York University Press</PUBLISHER>&n			<PUBCTYST>New York</PUBCTYST>&n			<PUBCNTRY>USA</PUBCNTRY>&n			<VOLUME/>&n			<ISSUE/>&n			<PAGENUM/>&n			<WEB_ADDRESS/>&n			<EDITORS>Min Zhou and Hasan Mahmud</EDITORS>&n			<ISBNISSN/>&n			<DOI/>&n			<AUDIENCE/>&n			<REFEREED/>&n			<EDITORIAL_REVIEWED/>&n			<PUBLICAVAIL/>&n			<ACTIVITY_RANK/>&n			<ACTIVITY_RANK_OTHER/>&n			<AREA>Human and Social Development</AREA>&n			<ABSTRACT>This chapter offers an overview of the changing trends of immigration from Latin America to the United States from 1990 to 2019. By combining data from a variety of sources to construct an up-to-date demographic and socioeconomic profile of Latino immigrants, the analysis contributes to the understanding of recent transformations of one of the largest waves of immigration in U.S. history. The available data yields a picture of significant changes in the characteristics and qualities of Latino immigrants as this robust migration phenomenon declines precipitously. Since the turn of the 21&amp;lt;sup&amp;gt;st&amp;lt;/sup&amp;gt;century, the foreign-born population from Latin America has shifted from a younger, male-dominated, recently arrived population to an older, more gender-balanced, and more settled resident population. While the overall magnitude of Latin American migrant is decreasing, the flow has become more diverse in terms of countries of origin and more selective with respect to education, especially since the Great Recession in 2008. What drives these trends is not merely the classic push-pull factors but the combination of demographic changes and levels of economic development in sending countries, racialized immigration control in the U.S. Descriptive analysis reveals that larger supplies of high-skilled immigrants contribute to enhancing the economic integration of Latino mmigrants. However, despite changes in cohort quality, the successful integration of Latino immigrants into the U.S. society is still hindered by the large presence of unskilled workers, whose path to legalization is blocked, low rates of naturalization among legal immigrants, and the ethno-racial profiling of U.S. immigration enforcement.</ABSTRACT>&n			<FULL_TEXT/>&n			<DTM_START/>&n			<DTD_START/>&n			<DTY_START>2020</DTY_START>&n			<START_START>2020-01-01</START_START>&n			<START_END>2020-12-31</START_END>&n			<DTM_SUB/>&n			<DTD_SUB/>&n			<DTY_SUB/>&n			<SUB_START/>&n			<SUB_END/>&n			<DTM_EXPSUB/>&n			<DTD_EXPSUB/>&n			<DTY_EXPSUB/>&n			<EXPSUB_START/>&n			<EXPSUB_END/>&n			<DTM_ACC/>&n			<DTY_ACC/>&n			<ACC_START/>&n			<ACC_END/>&n			<DTM_PUB/>&n			<DTD_PUB/>&n			<DTY_PUB>2022</DTY_PUB>&n			<PUB_START>2022-01-01</PUB_START>&n			<PUB_END>2022-12-31</PUB_END>&n			<USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>Yes</USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>&n		</INTELLCONT>&n		<INTELLCONT 1990-2021</TITLE>&n			<TITLE_SECONDARY>Cali-Tex-Mex: The International Relations of California and Texas with Mexico and the World</TITLE_SECONDARY>&n			<INTELLCONT_AUTH id="225449562113">&n				<FACULTY_NAME>1466528</FACULTY_NAME>&n				<FNAME>Rene</FNAME>&n				<MNAME>M</MNAME>&n				<LNAME>Zenteno</LNAME>&n				<STUDENT_LEVEL/>&n				<ROLE/>&n			</INTELLCONT_AUTH>&n			<INTELLCONT_AUTH Castro0</LNAME>&n				<STUDENT_LEVEL/>&n				<ROLE/>&n			</INTELLCONT_AUTH>&n			<PUBLISHER>Routledge</PUBLISHER>&n			<PUBCTYST>New York</PUBCTYST>&n			<PUBCNTRY>USA</PUBCNTRY>&n			<VOLUME/>&n			<ISSUE/>&n			<PAGENUM/>&n			<WEB_ADDRESS/>&n			<EDITORS/>&n			<ISBNISSN/>&n			<DOI/>&n			<AUDIENCE/>&n			<REFEREED/>&n			<EDITORIAL_REVIEWED/>&n			<PUBLICAVAIL/>&n			<ACTIVITY_RANK/>&n			<ACTIVITY_RANK_OTHER/>&n			<AREA>Human and Social Development</AREA>&n			<ABSTRACT>This chapter conducts a comparative analysis of California and Texas' diverging views and approaches in relation to policies aimed at immigrants' integration into society. It takes a closer look at the factors behind their dramatic shift in policy — California moving away from immigration enforcement toward “sanctuary” policies and Texas heading in the opposite direction. Policies to accommodate or hinder unauthorized migrants and refugees are a response to demographic changes. We document how and why, despite similar demographic transformations, California and Texas are giving rise to divergent policy positions concerning the inclusion or exclusion of ethnic minorities and immigrants. We argue that state policies in California and Texas largely reflect the current stance on immigration of the Democratic Party and the Republican Party, respectively. As the presence of Latinos and other minorities increases in the United States, the Democratic Party has reframed immigration in the context of sanctuary cities, with California leading the way. In contrast, Texas has become the most prominent face of the GOP on immigration enforcement. Our analysis sheds light on the potential future of the ongoing partisan polarization surrounding immigration, and its implication for the U.S.-Mexico relationship.   </ABSTRACT>&n			<FULL_TEXT/>&n			<DTM_START/>&n			<DTD_START/>&n			<DTY_START>2020</DTY_START>&n			<START_START>2020-01-01</START_START>&n			<START_END>2020-12-31</START_END>&n			<DTM_SUB/>&n			<DTD_SUB/>&n			<DTY_SUB/>&n			<SUB_START/>&n			<SUB_END/>&n			<DTM_EXPSUB/>&n			<DTD_EXPSUB/>&n			<DTY_EXPSUB/>&n			<EXPSUB_START/>&n			<EXPSUB_END/>&n			<DTM_ACC/>&n			<DTY_ACC/>&n			<ACC_START/>&n			<ACC_END/>&n			<DTM_PUB/>&n			<DTD_PUB/>&n			<DTY_PUB>2022</DTY_PUB>&n			<PUB_START>2022-01-01</PUB_START>&n			<PUB_END>2022-12-31</PUB_END>&n			<USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>Yes</USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>&n		</INTELLCONT>&n		<INTELLCONT dmd:created="2019-02-21T08:31:42" dmd:endDate="2019-12-31" dmd:lastModified="2021-09-15T11:03:15" dmd:lastModifiedSource="MANUAL" dmd:originalSource="MANUAL" dmd:startDate="2019-01-01" id="181654214656">&n			<CONTYPE>Book, Chapter in Scholarly Book-New</CONTYPE>&n			<CONTYPEOTHER/>&n			<CLASSIFICATION>Basic or Discovery Scholarship</CLASSIFICATION>&n			<PUBLICATION_LEVEL/>&n			<STATUS>Published</STATUS>&n			<TITLE>Recession vs. Removals, Which Finished Mexican Unauthorized Migration?</TITLE>&n			<TITLE_SECONDARY>Equitable Globalization? id="85482569729">&n				<FACULTY_NAME>1466528</FACULTY_NAME>&n				<FNAME>Rene</FNAME>&n				<MNAME>M</MNAME>&n				<LNAME>Zenteno</LNAME>&n				<STUDENT_LEVEL/>&n			</INTELLCONT_AUTH>&n			<INTELLCONT_AUTH Science</PUBLISHER>&n			<PUBCTYST/>&n			<PUBCNTRY/>&n			<VOLUME>648</VOLUME>&n			<ISSUE>1</ISSUE>&n			<PAGENUM>18-37</PAGENUM>&n			<WEB_ADDRESS/>&n			<EDITORS/>&n			<ISBNISSN/>&n			<DOI/>&n			<AUDIENCE/>&n			<REFEREED>Yes</REFEREED>&n			<EDITORIAL_REVIEWED/>&n			<PUBLICAVAIL>Yes</PUBLICAVAIL>&n			<ACTIVITY_RANK/>&n			<ACTIVITY_RANK_OTHER/>&n			<AREA>Human and Social Development</AREA>&n			<ABSTRACT>Traditional theories used to analyze international migration fail to distinguish age-related variations on their key propositions. This paper contributes to our understanding of how the motivation for migrating varies depending on the stage in the life course, particularly during the youth-to-adult transition. Using data from the Mexican Migration Project, we estimate discrete-time-hazard models of the probabilities of a first migration using individual, household, community and macroeconomic variables during and after adolescence, and for both men and women. Results indicate that the determinants of migration operate differently during adolescence than later in the life course. While migration-related social capital has proved to be important factor in increasing and perpetuating migration, we found that its effect is even stronger for teenagers. We also shed light on how adolescent migration is influenced by other major markers of the transition to adulthood such as education, labor force experience and family formation.</ABSTRACT>&n			<FULL_TEXT/>&n			<DTM_START/>&n			<DTD_START/>&n			<DTY_START/>&n			<START_START/>&n			<START_END/>&n			<DTM_SUB/>&n			<DTD_SUB/>&n			<DTY_SUB/>&n			<SUB_START/>&n			<SUB_END/>&n			<DTM_EXPSUB/>&n			<DTD_EXPSUB/>&n			<DTY_EXPSUB/>&n			<EXPSUB_START/>&n			<EXPSUB_END/>&n			<DTM_ACC/>&n			<DTY_ACC/>&n			<ACC_START/>&n			<ACC_END/>&n			<DTM_PUB/>&n			<DTD_PUB/>&n			<DTY_PUB>2013</DTY_PUB>&n			<PUB_START>2013-01-01</PUB_START>&n			<PUB_END>2013-12-31</PUB_END>&n			<USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>Yes</USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>&n		</INTELLCONT>&n		<PRESENT dmd:created="2021-09-15T13:18:37" dmd:lastModified="2021-09-15T22:36:50" dmd:lastModifiedSource="MANUAL" dmd:originalSource="MANUAL" dmd:startDate="2021-01-01" id="225560156160">&n			<TITLE>The Great Mexican Migration</TITLE>&n			<DESC>Between 1990 and 2000, about 6 million persons left Mexico for the United States, most of them entered and remain undocumented in this country. By its size and concentration, this stands as a singular event in the annals of contemporary migrations worldwide and unprecedented both in Mexico's experience as a sending country and the long history of immigration to the United States. This book will provide a comprehensive account of the conditions that created a "perfect storm" to propel this historical migration flow, the unintended consequences of U.S. immigration policies, and the lasting impacts of this unprecedented migration in U.S. society. &amp;lt;br&amp;gt; </DESC>&n			<RESPROG_COLL getXml:java.lang.String:<?xml version="1.0" encoding="UTF-8"?>&n<Data xmlns="http://www.digitalmeasures.com/schema/data" dmd:date="2022-01-21" xmlns:dmd="http://www.digitalmeasures.com/schema/data-metadata">&n	<Record dmd:surveyId="11977669" userId="1466528" username="ezs921">&n		<dmd:IndexEntry entryKey="FT" indexKey="ADMIN_FTPT" text="FT"/>&n		<dmd:IndexEntry entryKey="Professor" indexKey="ADMIN_RANK" text="Professor"/>&n		<dmd:IndexEntry entryKey="Tenured" indexKey="ADMIN_TENURE" text="Tenured"/>&n		<dmd:IndexEntry entryKey="College for Health, Community and Policy" indexKey="COLLEGE" text="College for Health, Community and Policy"/>&n		<dmd:IndexEntry entryKey="Demography" indexKey="DEPARTMENT" text="Demography"/>&n		<PCI dmd:created="2013-07-29T02:00:22" dmd:lastModified="2019-11-04T06:56:43" dmd:lastModifiedSource="SYSTEM" dmd:originalSource="IMPORT" id="75861690368">&n			<BANNER_ID>@01441749</BANNER_ID>&n			<EMPID>6001041047</EMPID>&n			<PIDM>1360224</PIDM>&n			<PREFIX/>&n			<FNAME>Rene</FNAME>&n			<MNAME>M</MNAME>&n			<LNAME>Zenteno</LNAME>&n			<SUFFIX/>&n			<FULL_NAME/>&n			<EMAIL>RENE.ZENTENO@UTSA.EDU</EMAIL>&n			<BUILDING>MONTEREY BUILDING</BUILDING>&n			<ROOMNUM>4.450B</ROOMNUM>&n			<OPHONE1>210</OPHONE1>&n			<OPHONE2>458</OPHONE2>&n			<OPHONE3>3140</OPHONE3>&n			<DPHONE1>210</DPHONE1>&n			<DPHONE2>458</DPHONE2>&n			<DPHONE3>3163</DPHONE3>&n			<FAX1>210</FAX1>&n			<FAX2>458</FAX2>&n			<FAX3>3164</FAX3>&n			<WEBSITE>http://copp.utsa.edu/demography/</WEBSITE>&n			<DTM_DOB>November</DTM_DOB>&n			<DTD_DOB>27</DTD_DOB>&n			<DTY_DOB>1962</DTY_DOB>&n			<DOB_START>1962-11-27</DOB_START>&n			<DOB_END>1962-11-27</DOB_END>&n			<GENDER>Male</GENDER>&n			<ETHNICITY>Hispanic or Latino</ETHNICITY>&n			<RACE>White</RACE>&n			<CITIZEN>No</CITIZEN>&n			<BIO>René Zenteno is currently professor of Demography in the College of Public Policy at the University of Texas at San Antonio. &n&nHe was the first vice provost for international initiatives at UTSA. During this time he was also the founding executive director of ConTex, a joint initiative of the University of Texas System and Mexico’s National Council of Science and Technology (CONACYT). As vice provost, he led efforts to advance the global profile and reputation of UTSA by: more than doubled the number of students participating in educational abroad experiences; expanding UTSA research collaborations and exchange programs for faculty and students with top ranked universities in Latin America, particularly Mexico, Brazil and Chile, as well as China, Japan, India, France and Germany; leading the first accreditation of UTSA’s Intensive English Program; and adopting streamlined procedures for travel to restricted regions. As executive director of ConTex, Zenteno spearheaded the launch of a $16 million five-year investment committed to fostering collaboration between the United States and Mexico through graduate training, research and discovery.&n&nZenteno served as the Under Secretary in the Ministry of the Interior in Mexico from 2010-2012. As Under Secretary, he oversaw all policies, programs and activities related to population, migration, and religious affairs. He was instrumental in writing, negotiating and enacting the 2011 Mexican Immigration Law. He also helped pass the Law on Refugees and Complementary Protection. These legal instruments provide basic rights to migrants and refugees in Mexico regardless of immigration status.&n&nPrior to joining the UTSA faculty, Zenteno served as provost and professor at El Colegio de la Frontera Norte and as executive director of the Center for U.S.-Mexican Studies at the University of California, San Diego. He also spent eight years on the faculty of the Monterrey Institute of Technology and Higher Education. &n&nOver the span of his academic career, Zenteno has procured 6.5 million dollars in grants, and has authored more than 50 publications. Zenteno has received several professional honors, including President of the Mexican Society of Demography (SOMEDE) and membership in the National Academy of Science of Mexico. He is a recipient of the Alfonso Reyes chair of the Institut des Hautes Etudes de l'Amérique latine at Université de la Sorbonne Nouvelle Paris III.&n&nZenteno has published widely in the areas of social and demographic change, international migration, and social inequality, with a focus on Mexico, U.S.-Mexican migration, and Mexican immigrant incorporation. Zenteno’s articles have been published in the Proceedings of the National Academic of Sciences, Social Problems, Journal of Development Economics, Latin American Research Review, International Migration Review, The Annals of the American Academy of Political and Social Science, Journal of Marriage and the Family, American Economic Journal: Applied Economics, Estudios Demográficos y Urbanos, Frontera Norte, and other academic journals.&n&nZenteno earned his bachelor’s degree in communications from Monterrey Institute of Technology and Higher Education, and his master’s degree in demography from El Colegio de Mexico. His doctorate is from the University of Texas at Austin in sociology and demography. He completed a two-year postdoctoral fellowship at the University of Pennsylvania. &n</BIO>&n			<TEACH_UNDERGRAD>Global Commerce &amp;amp; Human Mobility&nMarketing Research II&nSocio-cultural Values in Mexico and Latin America</TEACH_UNDERGRAD>&n			<TEACH_GRAD>International Migration&nPolicy Development&nGeneral Research Methods&nMexico: Development, Institutions and Policies&nEconomic Policy in Latin America&nQuantitative Methods&nDevelopment and Social Policy&nContemporary Sociology&nEpistemology&nInternational Migration&nResearch Methodology&nField Research&nStatistical Analysis of Survey Data&nMathematics and Research Methodology&nStatistics for Social Sciences&nSeminar on Social Methodology II&nPopulation and Society in Contemporary Mexico&nStatistics I&nApplied Statistics in Population Studies&nDemographic Analysis&nThesis Seminar&nSeminar on Migration I and II&nSeminar on Labor Force I and II&nSeminar on Population Change in Mexico&nDemographic Analysis&n&n&n&n&n</TEACH_GRAD>&n			<TEACHING_INTERESTS/>&n			<RESEARCH_INTERESTS>Migration is a major demographic force shaping the social, economic, political and cultural life of the United States and Mexico. Most of my research falls into three categories: 1) U.S.-Mexican migration, with special emphasis on its determinants and consequences for sending and receiving regions and immigrant adaptation, 2) immigration policy, undocumented migration and human rights and 3) social and demographic change in Mexico, including social mobility and family behavior. Underlying these foci is a common interest in issues of inequality, development, and stratification. A key tenet of my research is that social disparities in Mexico and the U.S. can be better understood by establishing comparisons between the two countries. This approach is relevant because millions of Mexicans today are shaping their life opportunities and those of their descendants by moving between two societies with clear divisions based on class and ethnicity. In order to understand the social inequality faced by Mexicans in both countries, I argue that we need to produce more systematic and comparable data and research on how characteristics of the family of origin are associated with educational, health and labor market outcomes on both sides of the border.</RESEARCH_INTERESTS>&n			<REFERENCES/>&n			<UPLOAD_PHOTO/>&n		</PCI>&n		<PASTHIST and Social Development</AREA>&n					<ABSTRACT>Traditional theories used to analyze international migration fail to distinguish age-related variations on their key propositions. This paper contributes to our understanding of how the motivation for migrating varies depending on the stage in the life course, particularly during the youth-to-adult transition. Using data from the Mexican Migration Project, we estimate discrete-time-hazard models of the probabilities of a first migration using individual, household, community and macroeconomic variables during and after adolescence, and for both men and women. Results indicate that the determinants of migration operate differently during adolescence than later in the life course. While migration-related social capital has proved to be important factor in increasing and perpetuating migration, we found that its effect is even stronger for teenagers. We also shed light on how adolescent migration is influenced by other major markers of the transition to adulthood such as education, labor force experience and family formation.</ABSTRACT>&n					<FULL_TEXT/>&n					<DTM_START/>&n					<DTD_START/>&n					<DTY_START/>&n					<START_START/>&n					<START_END/>&n					<DTM_SUB/>&n					<DTD_SUB/>&n					<DTY_SUB/>&n					<SUB_START/>&n					<SUB_END/>&n					<DTM_EXPSUB/>&n					<DTD_EXPSUB/>&n					<DTY_EXPSUB/>&n					<EXPSUB_START/>&n					<EXPSUB_END/>&n					<DTM_ACC/>&n					<DTY_ACC/>&n					<ACC_START/>&n					<ACC_END/>&n					<DTM_PUB/>&n					<DTD_PUB/>&n					<DTY_PUB>2013</DTY_PUB>&n					<PUB_START>2013-01-01</PUB_START>&n					<PUB_END>2013-12-31</PUB_END>&n					<USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>Yes</USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>&n				</INTELLCONT>&n			</PUBS>&n			<PRESENT dmd:startDate="2020-09-01" id="222851072000">&n			<TYPE_CONGRANT>Proposal</TYPE_CONGRANT>&n			<PROPOSAL_ID>10703</PROPOSAL_ID>&n			<TYPE>Subaward</TYPE>&n			<TITLE>Individual, Household, and Contextual Determinants of Maternal Health among U.S. Latinas: A National Cross-sectional and Local Area Prospective Study PI's/Co-PI's;Sharedcredit: Zenteno, Rene PI - 100.00%</TITLE>&n			<SPONORG>University of Pennsylvania</SPONORG>&n			<CONGRANT_INVEST dmd:primaryKey="1466528|Rene|M|Zenteno" id="222851072001">&n				<FACULTY_NAME>1466528</FACULTY_NAME>&n				<FNAME>Rene</FNAME>&n				<MNAME>M</MNAME>&n				<LNAME>Zenteno</LNAME>&n			</CONGRANT_INVEST>&n			<ROLE_PI>Lead PD/PI</ROLE_PI>&n			<AMOUNT>176785.00</AMOUNT>&n			<ABSTRACT>Following a social determinants of health framework, the overall aim of our proposal is to systematically assess and compare risk factors for maternal mortality among Latina women in the U.S., paying specific attention to variation by nativity, country of origin, and geographic context. We take a mixed-method approach, combining national level secondary data analysis with a prospective community based participatory research study in Philadelphia, PA that will follow women during and after their pregnancy to collect high quality data on health risks and outcomes. This type of comprehensive account is necessary to identify sociocultural protective factors and areas of resilience that can inform public health interventions and grassroots organizations oriented towards mitigating negative pregnancy health outcomes among Latinas. Our specific aims are to: 1- Using birth certificate data, describe and model individual and household level differences in maternal risks and morbidities (i.e., gestational diabetes; hypertension eclampsia; transfusion) as well as the intervening mechanisms shaping maternal health (i.e., access and adequacy of prenatal care and health insurance coverage). We will compare results to NH White, Black, and Asian women, both U.S. and foreign-born. 2- Linking birth certificate data with county level information on immigration and socioeconomic characteristics obtained from the American Community Survey, to formulate multilevel models to assess how ethnic community characteristics (i.e. size, growth, and gender composition of the Latino community) and socioeconomic contexts affect maternal health. We will compare results to NH White, Black, and Asian women, both U.S. and foreign-born. 3- Linking birth certificate data with county level information on health care resources obtained primarily from the County Health Rankings ; Roadmap (CHR) and Small Area Health Insurance Estimates Projects (SAHIE), to formulate multilevel models to assess how health care context affects maternal health. We will compare results to NH White, Black, and Asian women, both U.S. and foreign-born. 4- Develop jointly with the Centre for the Culture, Art, Training, and Education (CCATE) a community-based participatory research approach to the identification of maternal health issues, data collection, information, and dissemination with the potential to serve newly emerging local communities receiving Latinas. 5- Apply a mixed method data collection approach that will prospectively collect a quantitative survey through respondent-driven sampling and qualitative in-depth interviews on health behaviors, health access, and maternal health among Latina women during and after pregnancy in Philadelphia, PA.</ABSTRACT>&n			<STATUS>Submitted</STATUS>&n			<DTM_SUB>May</DTM_SUB>&n			<DTD_SUB>26</DTD_SUB>&n			<DTY_SUB>2020</DTY_SUB>&n			<SUB_START>2020-05-26</SUB_START>&n			<SUB_END>2020-05-26</SUB_END>&n			<DTM_START>September</DTM_START>&n			<DTD_START>01</DTD_START>&n			<DTY_START>2020</DTY_START>&n			<START_START>2020-09-01</START_START>&n			<START_END>2020-09-01</START_END>&n			<DTM_END>August</DTM_END>&n			<DTD_END>31</DTD_END>&n			<DTY_END>2025</DTY_END>&n			<END_START>2025-08-31</END_START>&n			<END_END>2025-08-31</END_END>&n			<USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>Yes</USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>&n		</CONGRANT>&n		<CONGRANT dmd:created="2021-07-23T17:38:40" dmd:endDate="2019-05-31" dmd:lastModified="2021-10-26T09:45:40" dmd:lastModifiedSource="SYSTEM" dmd:originalSource="IMPORT" dmd:primaryKey="5996" dmd:startDate="2016-06-01" id="222851067904">&n			<TYPE_CONGRANT>Proposal</TYPE_CONGRANT>&n			<PROPOSAL_ID>5996</PROPOSAL_ID>&n			<TYPE>Grant</TYPE>&n			<TITLE>Strategies of Transnational Mexican Adolescents: Immigrant Agency, Social Networks, and Incorporation PI's/Co-PI's;Sharedcredit: Romo, Harriett PI - 100.00% Christiansen, Martha Sidury Co-PI - 0.00% Zenteno, Rene Co-PI - 0.00% Jones, Richard Co-PI - 0.00%</TITLE>&n			<SPONORG>Natl Science Fdn</SPONORG>&n			<CONGRANT_INVEST dmd:primaryKey="1466528|Rene|M|Zenteno" id="222851067905">&n				<FACULTY_NAME>1466528</FACULTY_NAME>&n				<FNAME>Rene</FNAME>&n				<MNAME>M</MNAME>&n				<LNAME>Zenteno</LNAME>&n			</CONGRANT_INVEST>&n			<ROLE_PI>Co-PD/PI</ROLE_PI>&n			<AMOUNT>1000000.00</AMOUNT>&n			<ABSTRACT>Overview We propose to identify and explain the experiences of transnational Mexican-origin adolescents (14-18 year-olds with at least one Mexican parent, who have migrated between Mexico and the US) as they attempt to adjust to institutions that inhibit their incorporation into the host society. We specifically focus on two target populationsâ€”Mexican undocumented adolescents in the United States, and US-born Mexican adolescents living in Mexico. The former often do not discover their legal status until they are 16-18 years old, when they â€œawaken to a nightmareâ€&amp;#157; (Gonzales and Chavez 2012) in which they are unable to get a driver;s license, work legally or enter a university. As a result their school performance, aspirations and economic incorporation suffer. In the case of US-born adolescents in Mexico, these youth have returned to Mexico voluntarily or involuntarily, usually with their parents, where they find that Mexican institutions are poorly equipped to accept them (ZÃºÃ±iga and Hamman 2009). Our research calls upon the theories of balance, social networks and incorporation to probe how adolescents cope with these obstacles within the context of discrimination, arbitrary application of federal policies, inadequate school and family resources, language proficiency and other constraints. Our fully integrated interdisciplinary team at the University of Texas at San Antonio, including co-PIs from Sociology, Geography, Education and Demography, has identified three urban areas in Texas (San Antonio, Austin, and Houston/Pasadena) and in Mexico (Toluca, Puebla, and Leon) that have high numbers of our two target populations. After analyzing key attributes from the American Community Survey and the Mexican Family Life Survey, we will locate schools and classes likely to have high concentrations of our target populations. We will survey students in their classes on basic demographic characteristics. We will conduct life history interviews with a subset of students, including questions on their social networks, attitudes and behaviors. The data will be coded and analyzed using both quantitative and narrative research methods.</ABSTRACT>&n			<STATUS>Submitted</STATUS>&n			<DTM_SUB>December</DTM_SUB>&n			<DTD_SUB>01</DTD_SUB>&n			<DTY_SUB>2015</DTY_SUB>&n			<SUB_START>2015-12-01</SUB_START>&n			<SUB_END>2015-12-01</SUB_END>&n			<DTM_START>June</DTM_START>&n			<DTD_START>01</DTD_START>&n			<DTY_START>2016</DTY_START>&n			<START_START>2016-06-01</START_START>&n			<START_END>2016-06-01</START_END>&n			<DTM_END>May</DTM_END>&n			<DTD_END>31</DTD_END>&n			<DTY_END>2019</DTY_END>&n			<END_START>2019-05-31</END_START>&n			<END_END>2019-05-31</END_END>&n			<USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>Yes</USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>&n		</CONGRANT>&n		<CONGRANT dmd:created="2021-07-23T17:38:40" dmd:endDate="2015-08-31" dmd:lastModified="2021-10-26T09:45:40" dmd:lastModifiedSource="SYSTEM" dmd:originalSource="IMPORT" dmd:primaryKey="4341" dmd:startDate="2014-09-01" id="222851069952">&n			<TYPE_CONGRANT>Award</TYPE_CONGRANT>&n			<PROPOSAL_ID>4341</PROPOSAL_ID>&n			<TYPE>Grant</TYPE>&n			<TITLE>The Impact of Migration and Immigration on Mexican and Mexican-American Adolescents: A Transnational Study PI's/Co-PI's;Sharedcredit: Romo, Harriett PI - 75.00% Zenteno, Rene Co-PI - 25.00%</TITLE>&n			<SPONORG>UTSA VPR Office</SPONORG>&n			<CONGRANT_INVEST dmd:primaryKey="1466528|Rene|M|Zenteno" id="222851069953">&n				<FACULTY_NAME>1466528</FACULTY_NAME>&n				<FNAME>Rene</FNAME>&n				<MNAME>M</MNAME>&n				<LNAME>Zenteno</LNAME>&n			</CONGRANT_INVEST>&n			<ROLE_PI>Co-PD/PI</ROLE_PI>&n			<AMOUNT>20000.00</AMOUNT>&n			<AWARDED>20000.00</AWARDED>&n			<FUNDING>5000.00</FUNDING>&n			<ABSTRACT>ABSTRACT. The proposed collaboration will develop research proposals that explore educational, health, identity, and behavioral implications for Mexican and Mexican-origin youth, both in Mexico and the United States, resulting from transnational relocations and separations under the new economic and political realities of Mexico-U.S. relations. Our study includes: 1) children (in Mexico) of deported Mexican parents, 2) children (in Mexico) of voluntary returnees, and 3) children who remain in the United States of Mexican parents. Many youth with Mexican immigrant parents have been born in the United States and hold dual nationality. We are interested in what happens to those U.S.-citizen children growing up in Mexico who may eventually return to the United States. While the focus is on the impact of migration, the study also includes a comparison group of Mexican American and Mexican-born immigrant children in San Antonio, Texas and their developmental trajectory in an Hispanic-majority U.S. city. The interdisciplinary project involves scholars from social work, sociology, geography, history, bicultural-bilingual studies, and demography to capture the complexity of migration experiences. Each of the team members has conducted empirical research related to Mexican migration and/or Mexican and Mexican American youth in the United States. The team will collect baseline data on return migration and location of these youth using existing studies and demographic/census reports in the United States and Mexico. Working with Mexican collaborators and drawing from the methodological expertise and research paradigms of the disciplines represented in the collaboration, the team will develop and field test survey and interview instruments focusing on the role of migration experiences in child and youth development, identity construction, citizenship, family relations, and peer socialization in distinct contexts (i.e., rural Mexico, a Mexican northern-border city, a Southern Mexico indigenous community, and San Antonio, Texas). Our goal is to develop several research proposals to be submitted to foundations and agencies funding research on youth and migration processes. Each of the Department Chairs represented by the collaborators has agreed to provide additional support for the collaboration, including research assistance, travel funds, and supplies. The UTSA Mexico Center will house the collaboration.</ABSTRACT>&n			<STATUS>Funded</STATUS>&n			<DTM_SUB>February</DTM_SUB>&n			<DTD_SUB>15</DTD_SUB>&n			<DTY_SUB>2014</DTY_SUB>&n			<SUB_START>2014-02-15</SUB_START>&n			<SUB_END>2014-02-15</SUB_END>&n			<DTM_START>September</DTM_START>&n			<DTD_START>01</DTD_START>&n			<DTY_START>2014</DTY_START>&n			<START_START>2014-09-01</START_START>&n			<START_END>2014-09-01</START_END>&n			<DTM_END>August</DTM_END>&n			<DTD_END>31</DTD_END>&n			<DTY_END>2015</DTY_END>&n			<END_START>2015-08-31</END_START>&n			<END_END>2015-08-31</END_END>&n			<USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>Yes</USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>&n		</CONGRANT>&n		<INTELLCONT Scholarship</CLASSIFICATION>&n			<PUBLICATION_LEVEL/>&n			<STATUS>Submitted</STATUS>&n			<TITLE>Evolving Trends of Latin American Immigration in the United States: Challenges for the Integration of Skilled Immigrants</TITLE>&n			<TITLE_SECONDARY>Economic Migration to the United States</TITLE_SECONDARY>&n			<INTELLCONT_AUTH id="225548115969">&n				<FACULTY_NAME>1466528</FACULTY_NAME>&n				<FNAME>Rene</FNAME>&n				<MNAME>M</MNAME>&n				<LNAME>Zenteno</LNAME>&n				<STUDENT_LEVEL/>&n				<ROLE/>&n			</INTELLCONT_AUTH>&n			<PUBLISHER>New York University Press</PUBLISHER>&n			<PUBCTYST>New York</PUBCTYST>&n			<PUBCNTRY>USA</PUBCNTRY>&n			<VOLUME/>&n			<ISSUE/>&n			<PAGENUM/>&n			<WEB_ADDRESS/>&n			<EDITORS>Min Zhou and Hasan Mahmud</EDITORS>&n			<ISBNISSN/>&n			<DOI/>&n			<AUDIENCE/>&n			<REFEREED/>&n			<EDITORIAL_REVIEWED/>&n			<PUBLICAVAIL/>&n			<ACTIVITY_RANK/>&n			<ACTIVITY_RANK_OTHER/>&n			<AREA>Human and Social Development</AREA>&n			<ABSTRACT>This chapter offers an overview of the changing trends of immigration from Latin America to the United States from 1990 to 2019. By combining data from a variety of sources to construct an up-to-date demographic and socioeconomic profile of Latino immigrants, the analysis contributes to the understanding of recent transformations of one of the largest waves of immigration in U.S. history. The available data yields a picture of significant changes in the characteristics and qualities of Latino immigrants as this robust migration phenomenon declines precipitously. Since the turn of the 21&amp;lt;sup&amp;gt;st&amp;lt;/sup&amp;gt;century, the foreign-born population from Latin America has shifted from a younger, male-dominated, recently arrived population to an older, more gender-balanced, and more settled resident population. While the overall magnitude of Latin American migrant is decreasing, the flow has become more diverse in terms of countries of origin and more selective with respect to education, especially since the Great Recession in 2008. What drives these trends is not merely the classic push-pull factors but the combination of demographic changes and levels of economic development in sending countries, racialized immigration control in the U.S. Descriptive analysis reveals that larger supplies of high-skilled immigrants contribute to enhancing the economic integration of Latino mmigrants. However, despite changes in cohort quality, the successful integration of Latino immigrants into the U.S. society is still hindered by the large presence of unskilled workers, whose path to legalization is blocked, low rates of naturalization among legal immigrants, and the ethno-racial profiling of U.S. immigration enforcement.</ABSTRACT>&n			<FULL_TEXT/>&n			<DTM_START/>&n			<DTD_START/>&n			<DTY_START>2020</DTY_START>&n			<START_START>2020-01-01</START_START>&n			<START_END>2020-12-31</START_END>&n			<DTM_SUB/>&n			<DTD_SUB/>&n			<DTY_SUB/>&n			<SUB_START/>&n			<SUB_END/>&n			<DTM_EXPSUB/>&n			<DTD_EXPSUB/>&n			<DTY_EXPSUB/>&n			<EXPSUB_START/>&n			<EXPSUB_END/>&n			<DTM_ACC/>&n			<DTY_ACC/>&n			<ACC_START/>&n			<ACC_END/>&n			<DTM_PUB/>&n			<DTD_PUB/>&n			<DTY_PUB>2022</DTY_PUB>&n			<PUB_START>2022-01-01</PUB_START>&n			<PUB_END>2022-12-31</PUB_END>&n			<USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>Yes</USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>&n		</INTELLCONT>&n		<INTELLCONT 1990-2021</TITLE>&n			<TITLE_SECONDARY>Cali-Tex-Mex: The International Relations of California and Texas with Mexico and the World</TITLE_SECONDARY>&n			<INTELLCONT_AUTH id="225449562113">&n				<FACULTY_NAME>1466528</FACULTY_NAME>&n				<FNAME>Rene</FNAME>&n				<MNAME>M</MNAME>&n				<LNAME>Zenteno</LNAME>&n				<STUDENT_LEVEL/>&n				<ROLE/>&n			</INTELLCONT_AUTH>&n			<INTELLCONT_AUTH id="225449562114">&n				<FACULTY_NAME/>&n				<FNAME>Rafael</FNAME>&n				<MNAME/>&n				<LNAME>Fernández de Castro0</LNAME>&n				<STUDENT_LEVEL/>&n				<ROLE/>&n			</INTELLCONT_AUTH>&n			<PUBLISHER>Routledge</PUBLISHER>&n			<PUBCTYST>New York</PUBCTYST>&n			<PUBCNTRY>USA</PUBCNTRY>&n			<VOLUME/>&n			<ISSUE/>&n			<PAGENUM/>&n			<WEB_ADDRESS/>&n			<EDITORS/>&n			<ISBNISSN/>&n			<DOI/>&n			<AUDIENCE/>&n			<REFEREED/>&n			<EDITORIAL_REVIEWED/>&n			<PUBLICAVAIL/>&n			<ACTIVITY_RANK/>&n			<ACTIVITY_RANK_OTHER/>&n			<AREA>Human and Social Development</AREA>&n			<ABSTRACT>This chapter conducts a comparative analysis of California and Texas' diverging views and approaches in relation to policies aimed at immigrants' integration into society. It takes a closer look at the factors behind their dramatic shift in policy — California moving away from immigration enforcement toward “sanctuary” policies and Texas heading in the opposite direction. Policies to accommodate or hinder unauthorized migrants and refugees are a response to demographic changes. We document how and why, despite similar demographic transformations, California and Texas are giving rise to divergent policy positions concerning the inclusion or exclusion of ethnic minorities and immigrants. We argue that state policies in California and Texas largely reflect the current stance on immigration of the Democratic Party and the Republican Party, respectively. As the presence of Latinos and other minorities increases in the United States, the Democratic Party has reframed immigration in the context of sanctuary cities, with California leading the way. In contrast, Texas has become the most prominent face of the GOP on immigration enforcement. Our analysis sheds light on the potential future of the ongoing partisan polarization surrounding immigration, and its implication for the U.S.-Mexico relationship.   </ABSTRACT>&n			<FULL_TEXT/>&n			<DTM_START/>&n			<DTD_START/>&n			<DTY_START>2020</DTY_START>&n			<START_START>2020-01-01</START_START>&n			<START_END>2020-12-31</START_END>&n			<DTM_SUB/>&n			<DTD_SUB/>&n			<DTY_SUB/>&n			<SUB_START/>&n			<SUB_END/>&n			<DTM_EXPSUB/>&n			<DTD_EXPSUB/>&n			<DTY_EXPSUB/>&n			<EXPSUB_START/>&n			<EXPSUB_END/>&n			<DTM_ACC/>&n			<DTY_ACC/>&n			<ACC_START/>&n			<ACC_END/>&n			<DTM_PUB/>&n			<DTD_PUB/>&n			<DTY_PUB>2022</DTY_PUB>&n			<PUB_START>2022-01-01</PUB_START>&n			<PUB_END>2022-12-31</PUB_END>&n			<USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>Yes</USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>&n		</INTELLCONT>&n		<INTELLCONT dmd:created="2019-02-21T08:31:42" dmd:endDate="2019-12-31" dmd:lastModified="2021-09-15T11:03:15" dmd:lastModifiedSource="MANUAL" dmd:originalSource="MANUAL" dmd:startDate="2019-01-01" id="181654214656">&n			<CONTYPE>Book, Chapter in Scholarly Book-New</CONTYPE>&n			<CONTYPEOTHER/>&n			<CLASSIFICATION>Basic or Discovery Scholarship</CLASSIFICATION>&n			<PUBLICATION_LEVEL/>&n			<STATUS>Published</STATUS>&n			<TITLE>Recession vs. Removals, Which Finished Mexican Unauthorized Migration?</TITLE>&n			<TITLE_SECONDARY>Equitable Globalization? id="85482569729">&n				<FACULTY_NAME>1466528</FACULTY_NAME>&n				<FNAME>Rene</FNAME>&n				<MNAME>M</MNAME>&n				<LNAME>Zenteno</LNAME>&n				<STUDENT_LEVEL/>&n			</INTELLCONT_AUTH>&n			<INTELLCONT_AUTH Science</PUBLISHER>&n			<PUBCTYST/>&n			<PUBCNTRY/>&n			<VOLUME>648</VOLUME>&n			<ISSUE>1</ISSUE>&n			<PAGENUM>18-37</PAGENUM>&n			<WEB_ADDRESS/>&n			<EDITORS/>&n			<ISBNISSN/>&n			<DOI/>&n			<AUDIENCE/>&n			<REFEREED>Yes</REFEREED>&n			<EDITORIAL_REVIEWED/>&n			<PUBLICAVAIL>Yes</PUBLICAVAIL>&n			<ACTIVITY_RANK/>&n			<ACTIVITY_RANK_OTHER/>&n			<AREA>Human and Social Development</AREA>&n			<ABSTRACT>Traditional theories used to analyze international migration fail to distinguish age-related variations on their key propositions. This paper contributes to our understanding of how the motivation for migrating varies depending on the stage in the life course, particularly during the youth-to-adult transition. Using data from the Mexican Migration Project, we estimate discrete-time-hazard models of the probabilities of a first migration using individual, household, community and macroeconomic variables during and after adolescence, and for both men and women. Results indicate that the determinants of migration operate differently during adolescence than later in the life course. While migration-related social capital has proved to be important factor in increasing and perpetuating migration, we found that its effect is even stronger for teenagers. We also shed light on how adolescent migration is influenced by other major markers of the transition to adulthood such as education, labor force experience and family formation.</ABSTRACT>&n			<FULL_TEXT/>&n			<DTM_START/>&n			<DTD_START/>&n			<DTY_START/>&n			<START_START/>&n			<START_END/>&n			<DTM_SUB/>&n			<DTD_SUB/>&n			<DTY_SUB/>&n			<SUB_START/>&n			<SUB_END/>&n			<DTM_EXPSUB/>&n			<DTD_EXPSUB/>&n			<DTY_EXPSUB/>&n			<EXPSUB_START/>&n			<EXPSUB_END/>&n			<DTM_ACC/>&n			<DTY_ACC/>&n			<ACC_START/>&n			<ACC_END/>&n			<DTM_PUB/>&n			<DTD_PUB/>&n			<DTY_PUB>2013</DTY_PUB>&n			<PUB_START>2013-01-01</PUB_START>&n			<PUB_END>2013-12-31</PUB_END>&n			<USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>Yes</USER_REFERENCE_CREATOR>&n		</INTELLCONT>&n		<PRESENT dmd:created="2021-09-15T13:18:37" dmd:lastModified="2021-09-15T22:36:50" dmd:lastModifiedSource="MANUAL" dmd:originalSource="MANUAL" dmd:startDate="2021-01-01" id="225560156160">&n			<TITLE>The Great Mexican Migration</TITLE>&n			<DESC>Between 1990 and 2000, about 6 million persons left Mexico for the United States, most of them entered and remain undocumented in this country. By its size and concentration, this stands as a singular event in the annals of contemporary migrations worldwide and unprecedented both in Mexico's experience as a sending country and the long history of immigration to the United States. This book will provide a comprehensive account of the conditions that created a "perfect storm" to propel this historical migration flow, the unintended consequences of U.S. immigration policies, and the lasting impacts of this unprecedented migration in U.S. society. &amp;lt;br&amp;gt; </DESC>&n			<RESPROG_COLL
